<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Meat</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Animal flesh eaten as food</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Meat_(disambiguation)" title="Meat (disambiguation)">Meat (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FoodMeat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1916" data-file-width="2888" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/FoodMeat.jpg/220px-FoodMeat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/FoodMeat.jpg/330px-FoodMeat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/FoodMeat.jpg/440px-FoodMeat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FoodMeat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A selection of uncooked red meat and poultry</div></div></div>
<p><b>Meat</b> is <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animal</a> <a href="/wiki/Flesh" title="Flesh">flesh</a> that is eaten as <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup> Humans have hunted, farmed, and scavenged animals for meat since prehistoric times. The establishment of settlements in the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> allowed the <a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_animals" title="Domestication of animals">domestication of animals</a> such as chickens, sheep, rabbits, pigs, and cattle. This eventually led to their use in meat production on an industrial scale in <a href="/wiki/Slaughterhouse" title="Slaughterhouse">slaughterhouses</a>.
</p><p>Meat is mainly composed of water, <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">protein</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a>. It is edible raw, but is normally eaten after it has been cooked and seasoned or processed in a variety of ways. Unprocessed meat will <a href="/wiki/Meat_spoilage" title="Meat spoilage">spoil or rot</a> within hours or days as a result of infection with, and decomposition by, <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>.
</p><p>Meat is important to the <a href="/wiki/Food_industry" title="Food industry">food industry</a> and to economies and cultures around the world. There are nonetheless people who choose to not eat meat (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetarian" title="Vegetarian">vegetarians</a>) or any animal products (<a href="/wiki/Veganism" title="Veganism">vegans</a>), for reasons such as taste preferences, <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_eating_meat" title="Ethics of eating meat">ethics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_meat_production" title="Environmental impact of meat production">environmental concerns</a>, health concerns or religious dietary rules.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>
<p>The word <i>meat</i> comes from the <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> word <span title="Old English (ca. 450-1100)-language text"><i lang="ang">mete</i></span>, which referred to food in general. The term is related to <span title="Danish-language text"><i lang="da">mad</i></span> in <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a>, <span title="North Germanic languages collective text"><i lang="gmq">mat</i></span> in <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a>, and <span title="North Germanic languages collective text"><i lang="gmq">matur</i></span> in <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Faroese_language" title="Faroese language">Faroese</a>, which also mean 'food'. The word <span title="Old Frisian-language text"><i lang="ofs">mete</i></span> also exists in <a href="/wiki/Old_Frisian" title="Old Frisian">Old Frisian</a> (and to a lesser extent, modern <a href="/wiki/West_Frisian_language" title="West Frisian language">West Frisian</a>) to denote important food, differentiating it from <span title="Old Frisian-language text"><i lang="ofs">swiets</i></span> (sweets) and <span title="Old Frisian-language text"><i lang="ofs">dierfied</i></span> (animal feed).
</p><p>Most often, <i>meat</i> refers to <a href="/wiki/Skeletal_muscle" title="Skeletal muscle">skeletal muscle</a> and associated <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> and other tissues, but it may also describe other edible tissues such as <a href="/wiki/Offal" title="Offal">offal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1">: 1 </span></sup> <i>Meat</i> is sometimes also used in a more restrictive sense to mean the flesh of <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammalian</a> species (pigs, cattle, sheep, goats, etc.) raised and prepared for human consumption, to the exclusion of <a href="/wiki/Fish_as_food" title="Fish as food">fish</a>, other <a href="/wiki/Seafood" title="Seafood">seafood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Entomophagy" title="Entomophagy">insects</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a>, or other animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definition_of_MEAT_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Definition_of_MEAT-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the context of food, <i>meat</i> can also refer to "the edible part of something as distinguished from its covering (such as a husk or shell)", for example, <i>coconut meat</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Definition_of_MEAT_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Definition_of_MEAT-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>In English, there are also specialized terms for the meat of particular animals. These terms originated with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest of England</a> in 1066: while the animals retained their English names, their meat as brought to the tables of the invaders was referred to them with the <a href="/wiki/Norman_language" title="Norman language">Norman French</a> words for the respective animal. In time, these appellations came to be used by the entire population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hunting_and_farming">Hunting and farming</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">History of agriculture</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">Paleontological</a> evidence suggests that meat constituted a substantial proportion of the diet of the earliest humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2">: 2 </span></sup> Early <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a> depended on the organized hunting of large animals such as <a href="/wiki/Bison" title="Bison">bison</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2">: 2 </span></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a> of animals, of which we have evidence dating back to the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a> (c. 10,000 BCE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2">: 2 </span></sup> allowed the systematic production of meat and the <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">breeding</a> of animals with a view to improving meat production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 2">: 2 </span></sup> Animals that are now principal sources of meat were domesticated in conjunction with the development of early civilizations:
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lamb_meat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1019" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lamb_meat.jpg/220px-Lamb_meat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lamb_meat.jpg/330px-Lamb_meat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lamb_meat.jpg/440px-Lamb_meat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lamb_meat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical shoulder cut of <a href="/wiki/Lamb_and_mutton" title="Lamb and mutton">lamb</a></div></div></div>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">Sheep</a>, originating from western Asia, were domesticated with the help of dogs prior to the establishment of settled <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, likely as early as the 8th millennium BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 3">: 3 </span></sup> Several breeds of sheep were established in ancient <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> by 3500–3000 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 3">: 3 </span></sup> Today, more than 200 <a href="/wiki/List_of_sheep_breeds" title="List of sheep breeds">sheep-breeds</a> exist.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Cattle</a> were domesticated in Mesopotamia after settled agriculture was established about 5000 BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 5">: 5 </span></sup> and several breeds were established by 2500 BCE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 6">: 6 </span></sup> Modern domesticated cattle fall into the groups <i><a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">Bos taurus</a></i> (European cattle) and <i><a href="/wiki/Zebu" title="Zebu">Bos taurus indicus</a></i> (zebu), both descended from the now-extinct <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 5">: 5 </span></sup> The breeding of <a href="/wiki/Beef_cattle" title="Beef cattle">beef cattle</a>, cattle optimized for meat production as opposed to animals best suited for work or dairy purposes, began in the middle of the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-11"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 7">: 7 </span></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hereford_bull_large.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="369" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/220px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/330px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Hereford_bull_large.jpg/440px-Hereford_bull_large.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hereford_bull_large.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hereford_(cattle)" title="Hereford (cattle)">Hereford</a> bull, a breed of cattle frequently used in beef production.</div></div></div>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestic_pig" title="Domestic pig">Domestic pigs</a>, which are descended from <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boars</a>, are known to have existed about 2500 BCE in modern-day Hungary and in <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a>; earlier pottery from <a href="/wiki/Tell_es-Sultan" title="Tell es-Sultan">Tell es-Sultan</a> (Jericho) and Egypt depicts wild pigs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-12"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 8">: 8 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">Pork</a> sausages and <a href="/wiki/Ham" title="Ham">hams</a> were of great commercial importance in <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman</a> times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-13"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 8">: 8 </span></sup> Pigs continue to be bred intensively as they are being optimized to produce meat best suited for specific meat products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-14"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 9">: 9 </span></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">Goats</a> are among the earliest animals domesticated by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OKstate_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-OKstate-5">[5]</a></sup> The most recent genetic analysis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naderi_et_al._17659–17664_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naderi_et_al._17659–17664-6">[6]</a></sup> confirms the archaeological evidence that the wild <a href="/wiki/Bezoar_ibex" title="Bezoar ibex">bezoar ibex</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a> is the likely original ancestor of probably all domestic goats today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OKstate_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-OKstate-5">[5]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> farmers began to herd wild goats primarily for easy access to <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a> and meat, as well as to their dung, which was used as fuel; and their bones, hair, and sinew were used for clothing, building, and tools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hadog_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-hadog-7">[7]</a></sup> The earliest remnants of domesticated goats dating 10,000 years <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">Before Present</a> are found in <a href="/wiki/Ganj_Dareh" title="Ganj Dareh">Ganj Dareh</a> in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Goat remains have been found at archaeological sites in <a href="/wiki/Jericho" title="Jericho">Jericho</a>, <a href="/wiki/Choga_Mami" title="Choga Mami">Choga Mami</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Djeitun" title="Djeitun">Djeitun</a>, and <a href="/wiki/%C3%87ay%C3%B6n%C3%BC" title="Çayönü">Çayönü</a>, dating the domestication of goats in <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a> at between 8,000 and 9,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OKstate_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-OKstate-5">[5]</a></sup> Studies of DNA evidence suggests 10,000 years ago as the domestication date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naderi_et_al._17659–17664_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Naderi_et_al._17659–17664-6">[6]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">Chicken</a> were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domesticated" title="Domesticated">domesticated</a> around 6000 BC in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, according to genomic analysis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawal_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lawal-10">[10]</a></sup> and spread to China and India 2000–3000 years later. Archaeological evidence supports domestic chickens in Southeast Asia well before 6000 BC, China by 6000 BC and India by 2000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawal_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lawal-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dog_meat_at_Gyeongdong_Market.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Dog_meat_at_Gyeongdong_Market.jpg/220px-Dog_meat_at_Gyeongdong_Market.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Dog_meat_at_Gyeongdong_Market.jpg/330px-Dog_meat_at_Gyeongdong_Market.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Dog_meat_at_Gyeongdong_Market.jpg/440px-Dog_meat_at_Gyeongdong_Market.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dog_meat_at_Gyeongdong_Market.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dog meat sold at <a href="/wiki/Gyeongdong_Market" title="Gyeongdong Market">Gyeongdong Market</a>, Seoul, South Korea</div></div></div>
<p>Other animals are or have been raised or hunted for their flesh. The type of meat consumed varies much between different cultures, changes over time, depending on factors such as tradition and the availability of the animals. The amount and kind of meat consumed also varies by income, both between countries and within a given country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deer_meat" title="Deer meat">Deer</a> are hunted for their meat (<a href="/wiki/Venison" title="Venison">venison</a>) in various regions.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Horse_meat" title="Horse meat">Horses</a> are commonly eaten in France,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Italy, Germany and Japan, among other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Horses and other large <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Reindeer" title="Reindeer">reindeer</a> were hunted during the late <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> in western Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dog_meat" title="Dog meat">Dogs</a> are consumed in China,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> South Korea<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> and Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Dogs are also occasionally eaten in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Historically, dog meat has been consumed in various parts of the world, such as <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Japan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Switzerland<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> and Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cat_meat" title="Cat meat">Cats</a> are consumed in Southern China, Peru<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and sometimes also in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Italy" title="Northern Italy">Northern Italy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Guinea_pig" title="Guinea pig">Guinea pigs</a> are raised for their flesh in the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">Whales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a> are hunted, partly for their flesh, in Japan, <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, Canada, the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, Iceland, Saint Vincent and the Grenadines and by two small communities in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg/220px-SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg/330px-SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg/440px-SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SelectionOfPackageMeats.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fresh meat in a <a href="/wiki/Supermarket" title="Supermarket">supermarket</a> in North America</div></div></div>
<div class="PieChartTemplate thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px">
<div class="mw-no-invert" style="background-color:white;margin:auto;position:relative;width:200px;height:200px;overflow:hidden;border-radius:100px;border:1px solid black;transform:scaleX(-1)rotate(-90deg)">
<div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:0; top:0; border-width:0 200px 200px 0; border-color:green"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;left:100px; top:100px; border-width:100px 0 0 389.47428549299px; border-left-color:red"></div>

<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:blue"></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">Biomass</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a></b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:blue; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">Livestock</a>, mostly <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a> (60%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:red; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">Humans</a> (36%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:green; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Wildlife" title="Wildlife">Wild mammals</a> (4%)</div>
</div>
</div></div>
<p>Modern agriculture employs a number of techniques, such as <a href="/wiki/Progeny_testing" title="Progeny testing">progeny testing</a>, to speed <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">artificial selection</a> by breeding animals to rapidly acquire the qualities desired by meat producers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-15"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 10">: 10 </span></sup> For instance, in the wake of well-publicised health concerns associated with <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fats</a> in the 1980s, the fat content of United Kingdom beef, pork and lamb fell from 20–26 percent to 4–8 percent within a few decades, due to both selective breeding for leanness and changed methods of <a href="/wiki/Butcher" title="Butcher">butchery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-16"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 10">: 10 </span></sup> Methods of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> aimed at improving the meat production qualities of animals are now also becoming available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-17"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 14">: 14 </span></sup>
</p><p>Even though it is a very old industry, meat production continues to be shaped strongly by the evolving demands of customers. The trend towards selling meat in pre-packaged cuts has increased the demand for larger breeds of cattle, which are better suited to producing such cuts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-18"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 11">: 11 </span></sup> Even more animals not previously exploited for their meat are now being farmed, especially the more agile and mobile species, whose muscles tend to be developed better than those of cattle, sheep or pigs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-19"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 11">: 11 </span></sup> Examples are the various <a href="/wiki/Antelope" title="Antelope">antelope</a> species, the <a href="/wiki/Zebra" title="Zebra">zebra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">water buffalo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camel</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-20"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 11ff">: 11ff </span></sup> as well as non-mammals, such as the <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodile</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emu" title="Emu">emu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ostrich" title="Ostrich">ostrich</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-21"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 13">: 13 </span></sup> Another important trend in contemporary meat production is <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">organic farming</a> which, while providing no <a href="/wiki/Organoleptic" title="Organoleptic">organoleptic</a> benefit to meat so produced,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> meets an increasing demand for organic meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h3>
<p>For most of human history, meat was a largely unquestioned part of the human diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1">: 1 </span></sup>  Only in the 20th century did it begin to become a topic of discourse and contention in society, politics and wider culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 11">: 11 </span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumption">Consumption</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_meat_consumption_per_capita" title="List of countries by meat consumption per capita">List of countries by meat consumption per capita</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_meat_consumption" title="List of countries by meat consumption">List of countries by meat consumption</a></div>

</tbody></table><p>Meat consumption varies worldwide, depending on cultural or religious preferences, as well as economic conditions. <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism" title="Vegetarianism">Vegetarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Veganism" title="Veganism">vegans</a> choose not to eat meat because of taste preferences, ethical, economic, environmental, religious, or health concerns that are associated with meat production and consumption.

class="thumbcaption">Total annual meat consumption by type of meat</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>According to the analysis of the FAO, the overall consumption for <a href="#Red_and_white_meat">white meat</a> between 1990 and 2009 has dramatically increased. Poultry meat has increased by 76.6% per kilo per capita and pig meat by 19.7%. Bovine meat has decreased from 10.4 kg (22 lb 15 oz) per capita in 1990 to 9.6 kg (21 lb 3 oz) per capita in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Overall, diets that include meat are the most common worldwide according to the results of a 2018 <a href="/wiki/Ipsos_MORI" title="Ipsos MORI">Ipsos MORI</a> study of 16–64 years olds in 28 countries. Ipsos states "An omnivorous diet is the most common diet globally, with non-meat diets (which can include fish) followed by over a tenth of the global population." Approximately 87% of people include meat in their diet in some frequency. 73% of meat eaters included it in their diet regularly and 14% consumed meat only occasionally or infrequently. Estimates of the non-meat diets were also broken down. About 3% of people followed vegan diets, where consumption of meat, eggs, and dairy are abstained from. About 5% of people followed vegetarian diets, where consumption of meat is abstained from, but egg and/or dairy consumption is not strictly restricted. About 3% of people followed <a href="/wiki/Pescetarianism" title="Pescetarianism">pescetarian</a> diets, where consumption of the meat of land animals is abstained from, fish meat and other seafood is consumed, and egg and/or dairy consumption may or may not be strictly restricted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_2">History</span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Bioarchaeology" title="Bioarchaeology">bioarchaeological</a> (specifically, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotopic_analysis" title="Isotopic analysis">isotopic analysis</a>) study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_medieval_England" title="Early medieval England">early medieval England</a> found, based on the funerary record, that high-meat protein diets were extremely rare, and that (contrary to previously held assumptions) elites did not consume more meat than non-elites, and men did not consume more meat than women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the nineteenth century meat consumption in Britain was the highest in Europe, exceeded only by that in British colonies.  In the 1830s consumption per head in Britain was about 75 pounds a year, rising to 130 pounds in 1912. In 1904 laborers were found to consume 87 pounds a year while aristocrats ate 300 pounds. There were estimated to be 43,000 meat purveyor establishments in Britain in 1910, with "possibly more money invested in the meat industry than in any other British business" except the finance industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>  The US was a meat importing country by 1926.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Truncated lifespan as a result of intensive breeding allowed more meat to be produced from fewer animals. The world cattle population was about 600 million in 1929, with 700 million sheep and goats and 300 million pigs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> According to a study, the average lifespan of livestock pigs is ~2 years (7% of "<a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">maximum expected lifespan</a>"). For dairy cattle the lifespan is ~5 years (27%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1007/s11357-020-00190-4_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1007/s11357-020-00190-4-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_growth_and_development">Animal growth and development</span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Agricultural_science" title="Agricultural science">Agricultural science</a> has identified several factors bearing on the growth and development of meat in animals.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin:10px">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Trait
</th>
<th>Heritability<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Reproductive efficiency
</td>
<td>2–10%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Meat quality
</td>
<td>15–30%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Growth
</td>
<td>20–40%
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Muscle/fat ratio
</td>
<td>40–60%
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Several economically important traits in meat animals are heritable to some degree (see the adjacent table) and can thus be selected for by <a href="/wiki/Animal_breeding" title="Animal breeding">animal breeding</a>. In cattle, certain growth features are controlled by <a href="/wiki/Dominance_(genetics)" title="Dominance (genetics)">recessive genes</a> which have not so far been controlled, complicating breeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-22"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 18">: 18 </span></sup> One such trait is <a href="/wiki/Dwarfism" title="Dwarfism">dwarfism</a>; another is the doppelender or "<a href="/wiki/Myostatin" title="Myostatin">double muscling</a>" condition, which causes <a href="/wiki/Muscle_hypertrophy" title="Muscle hypertrophy">muscle hypertrophy</a> and thereby increases the animal's commercial value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-23"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 18">: 18 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Genetic_analysis" title="Genetic analysis">Genetic analysis</a> continues to reveal the genetic mechanisms that control numerous aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Endocrine_system" title="Endocrine system">endocrine system</a> and, through it, meat growth and quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-24"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 19">: 19 </span></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">Genetic engineering</a> techniques can shorten breeding programs significantly because they allow for the identification and isolation of <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> coding for desired traits, and for the reincorporation of these genes into the animal <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-25"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 21">: 21 </span></sup> To enable such manipulation, research is ongoing (as of 2006<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Meat&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>) to <a href="/wiki/Genome_project" title="Genome project">map the entire genome</a> of sheep, cattle and pigs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-26"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 21">: 21 </span></sup> Some research has already seen commercial application. For instance, a <a href="/wiki/Recombinant_DNA" title="Recombinant DNA">recombinant</a> <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterium</a> has been developed which improves the digestion of grass in the <a href="/wiki/Rumen" title="Rumen">rumen</a> of cattle, and some specific features of muscle fibres have been genetically altered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-27"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 22">: 22 </span></sup>
</p><p>Experimental <a href="/wiki/Cloning" title="Cloning">reproductive cloning</a> of commercially important meat animals such as sheep, pig or cattle has been successful. Multiple asexual reproduction of animals bearing desirable traits is anticipated,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-28"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 22">: 22 </span></sup> although this is not yet practical on a commercial scale.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span></h3>
<p>Heat regulation in <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> is of great economic significance, because mammals attempt to maintain a constant optimal body temperature. Low temperatures tend to prolong animal development and high temperatures tend to retard it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-29"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 22">: 22 </span></sup> Depending on their size, body shape and insulation through tissue and fur, some animals have a relatively narrow zone of temperature tolerance and others (e.g. cattle) a broad one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-30"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 23">: 23 </span></sup> Static <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a>, for reasons still unknown, also retard animal development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-31"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 23">: 23 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span></h3>
<p>The quality and quantity of usable meat depends on the animal's <i>plane of nutrition</i>, i.e., whether it is over- or underfed. Scientists disagree about how exactly the plane of nutrition influences carcass composition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-32"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 25">: 25 </span></sup>
</p><p>The composition of the diet, especially the amount of protein provided, is also an important factor regulating animal growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-33"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 26">: 26 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ruminant" title="Ruminant">Ruminants</a>, which may digest <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>, are better adapted to poor-quality diets, but their ruminal microorganisms degrade high-quality protein if supplied in excess.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-34"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 27">: 27 </span></sup> Because producing high-quality protein animal feed is expensive (see also <i><a href="#Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</a></i> below), several techniques are employed or experimented with to ensure maximum utilization of protein. These include the treatment of feed with <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formalin</a> to protect <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a> during their passage through the <a href="/wiki/Rumen" title="Rumen">rumen</a>, the recycling of <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">manure</a> by feeding it back to cattle mixed with feed concentrates, or the partial conversion of <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> to protein through microbial action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-35"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 30">: 30 </span></sup>
</p><p>In plant feed, environmental factors influence the availability of crucial <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> or <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a>, a lack or excess of which can cause a great many ailments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-36"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 29">: 29 </span></sup> In Australia, for instance, where the soil contains limited <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphate</a>, cattle are being fed additional phosphate to increase the efficiency of beef production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-37"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 28">: 28 </span></sup> Also in Australia, cattle and sheep in certain areas were often found losing their appetite and dying in the midst of rich pasture; this was at length found to be a result of <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> deficiency in the soil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-38"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 29">: 29 </span></sup> Plant <a href="/wiki/Toxin" title="Toxin">toxins</a> are also a risk to grazing animals; for instance, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_fluoroacetate" title="Sodium fluoroacetate">sodium fluoroacetate</a>, found in some African and Australian plants, kills by disrupting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cellular_metabolism" title="Cellular metabolism">cellular metabolism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-39"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 29">: 29 </span></sup> Certain man-made <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">pollutants</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Methylmercury" title="Methylmercury">methylmercury</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticide</a> residues present a particular hazard due to their tendency to <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioaccumulate</a> in meat, potentially poisoning consumers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-40"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 30">: 30 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_welfare">Animal welfare</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Animal_Welfare_Labelling" title="Animal Welfare Labelling">Animal Welfare Labelling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Standardization" title="Standardization">Standardization</a></div>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare#Farmed_animals" title="Animal welfare">Animal welfare § Farmed animals</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Animal_welfare&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:254px;max-width:254px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Animal_Abuse_Battery_Cage_01.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="427" data-file-width="570" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Animal_Abuse_Battery_Cage_01.jpg/250px-Animal_Abuse_Battery_Cage_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Animal_Abuse_Battery_Cage_01.jpg/375px-Animal_Abuse_Battery_Cage_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Animal_Abuse_Battery_Cage_01.jpg/500px-Animal_Abuse_Battery_Cage_01.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Free_Range_Hens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_342791.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Free_Range_Hens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_342791.jpg/250px-Free_Range_Hens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_342791.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Free_Range_Hens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_342791.jpg/375px-Free_Range_Hens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_342791.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Free_Range_Hens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_342791.jpg/500px-Free_Range_Hens_-_geograph.org.uk_-_342791.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:252px;max-width:252px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Free-range-hens.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Free-range-hens.jpg/250px-Free-range-hens.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Free-range-hens.jpg/375px-Free-range-hens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Free-range-hens.jpg/500px-Free-range-hens.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">The welfare of egg laying hens in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battery_cages" title="Battery cages">battery cages</a> (top) can be compared with the welfare of <a href="/wiki/Free_range" title="Free range">free range</a> hens (middle and bottom) which are given access to the outdoors. However, animal welfare groups argue that the vast majority of free-range hens are still intensively confined (bottom) and are rarely able to go outdoors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>A major concern for the welfare of farmed animals is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Factory_farming" title="Factory farming">factory farming</a> in which large numbers of animals are reared in confinement at high stocking densities. Issues include the limited opportunities for natural behaviors, for example, in <a href="/wiki/Battery_cage" title="Battery cage">battery cages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veal" title="Veal">veal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gestation_crate" title="Gestation crate">gestation crates</a>, instead producing <a href="/wiki/List_of_abnormal_behaviours_in_animals" title="List of abnormal behaviours in animals">abnormal behaviors</a> such as tail-biting, cannibalism, and <a href="/wiki/Abnormal_behaviour_of_birds_in_captivity" title="Abnormal behaviour of birds in captivity">feather pecking</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Overview_of_discretionary_invasive_procedures_on_animals" title="Overview of discretionary invasive procedures on animals">routine invasive procedures</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Debeaking" title="Debeaking">beak trimming</a>, <a href="/wiki/Castration" title="Castration">castration</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Animal_identification" title="Animal identification">ear notching</a>. More extensive methods of farming, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Free_range" title="Free range">free range</a>, can also raise welfare concerns such as the <a href="/wiki/Mulesing" title="Mulesing">mulesing</a> of sheep, predation of stock by wild animals, and <a href="/wiki/Biosecurity" title="Biosecurity">biosecurity</a>.
</p><p>Farmed animals are artificially selected for production parameters which sometimes impinge on the animals' welfare. For example, <a href="/wiki/Broiler" title="Broiler">broiler</a> chickens are bred to be very large to produce the greatest quantity of meat per animal. Broilers bred for fast growth have a high incidence of leg deformities because the large breast muscles cause distortions of the developing legs and pelvis, and the birds cannot support their increased body weight. As a consequence, they frequently become lame or suffer from broken legs. The increased body weight also puts a strain on their hearts and lungs, and <a href="/wiki/Ascites" title="Ascites">ascites</a> often develops. In the UK alone, up to 20 million broilers each year die from the stress of catching and transport before reaching the slaughterhouse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
Another concern about the welfare of farmed animals is the method of <a href="/wiki/Animal_slaughter" title="Animal slaughter">slaughter</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Ritual_slaughter" title="Ritual slaughter">ritual slaughter</a>. While the killing of animals need not necessarily involve suffering, the general public considers that killing an animal reduces its welfare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> This leads to further concerns about premature slaughtering such as <a href="/wiki/Chick_culling" title="Chick culling">chick culling</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Poultry_farming" title="Poultry farming">laying hen industry</a>, in which males are slaughtered immediately after hatching because they are superfluous; this policy occurs in other farmed animal industries such as the production of goat and cattle milk, raising the same concerns.</div></div>
<p>Livestock animals have shown relatively high intelligence which may raise <a href="/wiki/Animal_ethics" title="Animal ethics">animal ethics</a> rationale for safeguarding their well-being. Pigs in particular are considered by some to be the smartest known domesticated animal in the world<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> (e.g. more intelligent than pet dogs)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> which not only experience pain<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> but also have notable depths, levels and/or variety/diversity of emotions (including boredom),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> cognition, intelligence, and/or sentience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Complications include that without or reduced meat production, many livestock animals may never live (see also: <a href="/wiki/Natalism" title="Natalism">natalism</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> and that their life (relative timespan of existence) is typically short – in the case of pigs ~7% of their "maximum expected lifespan".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1007/s11357-020-00190-4_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1007/s11357-020-00190-4-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_intervention">Human intervention</span></h3>
<p>Meat producers may seek to improve the <a href="/wiki/Fertility" title="Fertility">fertility</a> of female animals through the administration of <a href="/wiki/Gonadotropic_cell" title="Gonadotropic cell">gonadotrophic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ovulation" title="Ovulation">ovulation</a>-inducing <a href="/wiki/Hormone" title="Hormone">hormones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-41"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 31">: 31 </span></sup> In pig production, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domestic_pig" title="Domestic pig">sow</a> infertility is a common problem — possibly due to excessive fatness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-42"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 32">: 32 </span></sup> No methods currently exist to augment the fertility of male animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-43"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 32">: 32 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Artificial_insemination" title="Artificial insemination">Artificial insemination</a> is now routinely used to produce animals of the best possible genetic quality, and the efficiency of this method is improved through the administration of hormones that synchronize the ovulation cycles within groups of females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-44"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 33">: 33 </span></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Growth_hormone" title="Growth hormone">Growth hormones</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Anabolism" title="Anabolism">anabolic</a> agents such as <a href="/wiki/Steroid" title="Steroid">steroids</a>, are used in some countries to accelerate muscle growth in animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-45"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 33">: 33 </span></sup> This practice has given rise to the <a href="/wiki/Beef_hormone_controversy" title="Beef hormone controversy">beef hormone controversy</a>, an international trade dispute. It may also decrease the tenderness of meat, although research on this is inconclusive,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-46"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 35">: 35 </span></sup> and have other effects on the composition of the muscle flesh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-47"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 36ff">: 36ff </span></sup> Where <a href="/wiki/Castration" title="Castration">castration</a> is used to improve control over male animals, its side effects are also counteracted by the administration of hormones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-48"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 33">: 33 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Myostatin" title="Myostatin">Myostatin</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Myostatin-related_muscle_hypertrophy" title="Myostatin-related muscle hypertrophy">muscle hypertrophy</a> has also been used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sedative" title="Sedative">Sedatives</a> may be administered to animals to counteract stress factors and increase weight gain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-49"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 39">: 39 </span></sup> The feeding of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotics" title="Antibiotics">antibiotics</a> to certain animals has been shown to improve growth rates also.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-50"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 39">: 39 </span></sup> This practice is particularly prevalent in the US, but has been banned in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">EU</a>, partly because it causes <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_resistance" title="Antimicrobial resistance">antimicrobial resistance</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pathogen" title="Pathogen">pathogenic</a> microorganisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-51"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 39">: 39 </span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biochemical_composition">Biochemical composition</span></h2>
<p>Numerous aspects of the biochemical composition of meat vary in complex ways depending on the species, breed, sex, age, plane of nutrition, training and exercise of the animal, as well as on the anatomical location of the musculature involved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-52"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 94–126">: 94–126 </span></sup> Even between animals of the same litter and sex there are considerable differences in such parameters as the percentage of intramuscular fat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-53"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 126">: 126 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Main_constituents">Main constituents</span></h3>
<p>Adult mammalian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a> flesh consists of roughly 75 percent water, 19 percent protein, 2.5 percent intramuscular fat, 1.2 percent <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and 2.3 percent other soluble non-protein substances. These include <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogenous</a> compounds, such as <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acids</a>, and inorganic substances such as minerals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-54"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 76">: 76 </span></sup>
</p><p>Muscle proteins are either soluble in water (<a href="/wiki/Sarcoplasm" title="Sarcoplasm">sarcoplasmic</a> proteins, about 11.5 percent of total muscle mass) or in concentrated salt solutions (<a href="/wiki/Myofibril" title="Myofibril">myofibrillar</a> proteins, about 5.5 percent of mass).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-55"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 75">: 75 </span></sup> There are several hundred sarcoplasmic proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-56"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 77">: 77 </span></sup> Most of them – the glycolytic <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> – are involved in the <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolytic pathway</a>, i.e., the conversion of stored energy into muscle power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-57"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 78">: 78 </span></sup> The two most abundant myofibrillar proteins, <a href="/wiki/Myosin" title="Myosin">myosin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Actin" title="Actin">actin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-58"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 79">: 79 </span></sup> are responsible for the muscle's overall structure. The remaining protein mass consists of <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a> (<a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elastin" title="Elastin">elastin</a>) as well as <a href="/wiki/Organelle" title="Organelle">organelle</a> tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-59"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 79">: 79 </span></sup>
</p><p>Fat in meat can be either <a href="/wiki/Adipose_tissue" title="Adipose tissue">adipose tissue</a>, used by the animal to store energy and consisting of "true fats" (<a href="/wiki/Ester" title="Ester">esters</a> of <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a> with <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-60"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 82">: 82 </span></sup> or intramuscular fat, which contains considerable quantities of <a href="/wiki/Phospholipid" title="Phospholipid">phospholipids</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Saponification" title="Saponification">unsaponifiable</a> constituents such as <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-61"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 82">: 82 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Red_and_white">Red and white</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blade_steak.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Blade_steak.jpg/220px-Blade_steak.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Blade_steak.jpg/330px-Blade_steak.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Blade_steak.jpg/440px-Blade_steak.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blade_steak.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Blade steaks are an example of "red" meat.</div></div></div>
<p>Meat can be broadly classified as "red" or "white" depending on the concentration of <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a> in muscle fibre. When myoglobin is exposed to <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, reddish oxymyoglobin develops, making myoglobin-rich meat appear red. The redness of meat depends on species, animal age, and fibre type: <a href="/wiki/Red_meat" title="Red meat">Red meat</a> contains more narrow muscle fibres that tend to operate over long periods without rest,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-62"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 93">: 93 </span></sup> while <a href="/wiki/White_meat" title="White meat">white meat</a> contains more broad fibres that tend to work in short fast bursts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-63"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 93">: 93 </span></sup>
</p><p>Generally, the meat of adult mammals such as <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">cows</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lamb_and_mutton" title="Lamb and mutton">sheep</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Horse_meat" title="Horse meat">horses</a> is considered red, while <a href="/wiki/Chicken_as_food" title="Chicken as food">chicken</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkey_meat" title="Turkey meat">turkey</a> breast meat is considered white.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutritional_information">Nutritional information</span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right; margin-left:1em;">

<p>All <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a> tissue is very high in protein, containing all of the <a href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acid" title="Essential amino acid">essential amino acids</a>, and in most cases is a good source of <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B12" title="Vitamin B12">vitamin B<sub>12</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">selenium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niacin_(substance)" title="Niacin (substance)">niacin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B6" title="Vitamin B6">vitamin B<sub>6</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Choline" title="Choline">choline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riboflavin" title="Riboflavin">riboflavin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beef.org_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-beef.org-67">[67]</a></sup> Several forms of meat are also high in <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_K" title="Vitamin K">vitamin K</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k2_foods_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-k2_foods-68">[68]</a></sup> Muscle tissue is very low in carbohydrates and does not contain dietary fiber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> While taste quality may vary between meats, the proteins, vitamins, and minerals available from meats are generally consistent.
</p><p>The fat content of meat can vary widely depending on the <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> and <a href="/wiki/Breed" title="Breed">breed</a> of animal, the way in which the animal was raised, including what it was fed, the <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomical</a> part of the body, and the methods of butchering and cooking. Wild animals such as <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a> are typically leaner than farm animals, leading those concerned about fat content to choose <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_(food)" title="Game (food)">game</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Venison" title="Venison">venison</a>. Decades of breeding meat animals for fatness is being reversed by consumer demand for meat with less fat. The fatty deposits that exist with the muscle fibers in meats soften meat when it is cooked and improve the flavor through chemical changes initiated through heat that allow the protein and fat molecules to interact. The fat, when cooked with meat, also makes the meat seem juicier. The nutritional contribution of the fat is mainly calories as opposed to protein. As fat content rises, the meat's contribution to nutrition declines. In addition, there is <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> associated with fat surrounding the meat. The cholesterol is a lipid associated with the kind of saturated fat found in meat. The increase in meat consumption after 1960 is associated with, though not definitively the cause of, significant imbalances of fat and cholesterol in the human diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>The table in this section compares the nutritional content of several types of meat. While each kind of meat has about the same content of protein and carbohydrates, there is a very wide range of fat content.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" slaughter">Animal slaughter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slaughterhouse" title="Slaughterhouse">Slaughterhouse</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Butchery" title="Butchery">Butchery</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fishing_industry" title="Fishing industry">Fishing industry</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atria_slaughterhouse_in_Nurmo_Seinajoki.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Atria_slaughterhouse_in_Nurmo_Seinajoki.JPG/220px-Atria_slaughterhouse_in_Nurmo_Seinajoki.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Atria_slaughterhouse_in_Nurmo_Seinajoki.JPG/330px-Atria_slaughterhouse_in_Nurmo_Seinajoki.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Atria_slaughterhouse_in_Nurmo_Seinajoki.JPG/440px-Atria_slaughterhouse_in_Nurmo_Seinajoki.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atria_slaughterhouse_in_Nurmo_Seinajoki.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Slaughterhouse" title="Slaughterhouse">slaughterhouse</a> of the meat company <a href="/wiki/Atria_(company)" title="Atria (company)">Atria</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sein%C3%A4joki" title="Seinäjoki">Seinäjoki</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Ostrobothnia" title="South Ostrobothnia">South Ostrobothnia</a>, Finland</div></div></div>
<p>Meat is produced by killing an animal and cutting flesh out of it. These procedures are called <a href="/wiki/Animal_slaughter" title="Animal slaughter">slaughter</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Butchery" title="Butchery">butchery</a>, respectively. There is ongoing research into producing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In_vitro_meat" title="In vitro meat">meat <i>in vitro</i></a>; that is, outside of animals.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span></h3>
<p>Upon reaching a predetermined age or weight, livestock are usually transported <i>en masse</i> to the slaughterhouse. Depending on its length and circumstances, this may exert stress and injuries on the animals, and some may die <i>en route</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-64"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 129">: 129 </span></sup> Unnecessary stress in transport may adversely affect the quality of the meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-65"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 129">: 129 </span></sup> In particular, the muscles of stressed animals are low in water and <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>, and their <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> fails to attain acidic values, all of which results in poor meat quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-66"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 130">: 130 </span></sup> Consequently, and also due to campaigning by <a href="/wiki/Animal_welfare" title="Animal welfare">animal welfare</a> groups, laws and industry practices in several countries tend to become more restrictive with respect to the duration and other circumstances of livestock transports.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slaughter">Slaughter</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Animal_slaughter" title="Animal slaughter">Animal slaughter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meat_industry" title="Meat industry">Meat industry</a></div>
<p>Animals are usually slaughtered by being first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stunned" title="Stunned">stunned</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Exsanguination" title="Exsanguination">exsanguinated</a> (bled out). Death results from the one or the other procedure, depending on the methods employed. Stunning can be effected through <a href="/wiki/Asphyxia" title="Asphyxia">asphyxiating</a> the animals with <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, shooting them with a <a href="/wiki/Gun" title="Gun">gun</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Captive_bolt_pistol" title="Captive bolt pistol">captive bolt pistol</a>, or shocking them with electric current.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-67"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 134ff">: 134ff </span></sup> In most forms of <a href="/wiki/Ritual_slaughter" title="Ritual slaughter">ritual slaughter</a>, stunning is not allowed.
</p><p>Draining as much blood as possible from the carcass is necessary because blood causes the meat to have an unappealing appearance and is a breeding ground for microorganisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-68"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1340">: 1340 </span></sup> The exsanguination is accomplished by severing the <a href="/wiki/Carotid_artery" title="Carotid artery">carotid artery</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jugular_vein" title="Jugular vein">jugular vein</a> in cattle and sheep, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anterior_vena_cava" title="Anterior vena cava">anterior vena cava</a> in pigs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-69"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 137">: 137 </span></sup>
</p><p>The act of slaughtering animals for meat, or of raising or transporting animals for slaughter, may engender both psychological stress<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and physical trauma<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> in the people involved. Additionally, slaughterhouse workers are exposed to noise of between 76 and 100 <a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">dB</a> from the screams of animals being killed. 80 dB is the threshold at which the wearing of <a href="/wiki/Ear_protection" title="Ear protection">ear protection</a> is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dressing_and_cutting">Dressing and cutting</span></h3>
<p>After exsanguination, the carcass is dressed; that is, the head, feet, hide (except hogs and some veal), excess fat, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viscera" title="Viscera">viscera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Offal" title="Offal">offal</a> are removed, leaving only bones and edible muscle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-70"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 138">: 138 </span></sup> Cattle and pig carcases, but not those of sheep, are then split in half along the mid ventral axis, and the carcase is cut into wholesale pieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-71"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 138">: 138 </span></sup> The dressing and cutting sequence, long a province of manual labor, is progressively being fully automated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-72"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 138">: 138 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conditioning">Conditioning</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2866" data-file-width="4201" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg/220px-MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg/330px-MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg/440px-MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MIN_Rungis_viandes_de_boucherie_veau.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the meat products sector of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/March%C3%A9_d%27Int%C3%A9r%C3%AAt_National_de_Rungis" title="Marché d'Intérêt National de Rungis">Rungis International Market</a>, France</div></div></div>
<p>Under hygienic conditions and without other treatment, meat can be stored at above its freezing point (–1.5 °C) for about six weeks without spoilage, during which time it undergoes an aging process that increases its tenderness and flavor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-73"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 141">: 141 </span></sup>
</p><p>During the first day after death, <a href="/wiki/Glycolysis" title="Glycolysis">glycolysis</a> continues until the accumulation of <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic acid</a> causes the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> to reach about 5.5.
