The remaining <a href="/wiki/Glycogen" title="Glycogen">glycogen</a>, about 18 g per kg, is believed to increase the water-holding capacity and tenderness of the flesh when cooked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-74"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 87">: 87 </span></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Rigor_mortis" title="Rigor mortis">Rigor mortis</a></i> sets in a few hours after death as <a href="/wiki/Adenosine_triphosphate" title="Adenosine triphosphate">ATP</a> is used up, causing <a href="/wiki/Actin" title="Actin">actin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myosin" title="Myosin">myosin</a> to combine into rigid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Actomyosin" title="Actomyosin">actomyosin</a> and lowering the meat's water-holding capacity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-75"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 90">: 90 </span></sup> causing it to lose water ("weep").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-76"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 146">: 146 </span></sup> In muscles that enter <i>rigor</i> in a contracted position, actin and myosin filaments overlap and cross-bond, resulting in meat that is tough on cooking<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-77"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 144">: 144 </span></sup> – hence again the need to prevent pre-slaughter stress in the animal.
</p><p>Over time, the muscle proteins <a href="/wiki/Denaturation_(biochemistry)" title="Denaturation (biochemistry)">denature</a> in varying degree, with the exception of the collagen and <a href="/wiki/Elastin" title="Elastin">elastin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Connective_tissue" title="Connective tissue">connective tissue</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-78"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 142">: 142 </span></sup> and <i>rigor mortis</i> resolves. Because of these changes, the meat is tender and pliable when cooked just after death or after the resolution of <i>rigor</i>, but tough when cooked during <i>rigor.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-79"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 142">: 142 </span></sup> As the muscle pigment <a href="/wiki/Myoglobin" title="Myoglobin">myoglobin</a> denatures, its iron <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidates</a>, which may cause a brown discoloration near the surface of the meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-80"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 146">: 146 </span></sup> Ongoing <a href="/wiki/Proteolysis" title="Proteolysis">proteolysis</a> also contributes to conditioning. <a href="/wiki/Hypoxanthine" title="Hypoxanthine">Hypoxanthine</a>, a breakdown product of ATP, contributes to the meat's flavor and odor, as do other products of the decomposition of muscle fat and protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_1-81"><a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 155">: 155 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Additives">Additives</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sucuk-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1386" data-file-width="1824" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Sucuk-1.jpg/220px-Sucuk-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Sucuk-1.jpg/330px-Sucuk-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Sucuk-1.jpg/440px-Sucuk-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sucuk-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The word "<a href="/wiki/Sausage" title="Sausage">sausage</a>" is derived from <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <span title="Old French (842-ca. 1400)-language text"><i lang="fro">saussiche</i></span>, from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> word <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">salsus</i></span><span class="lang-comment" style="font-style: normal; display: none; color: #33aa33; margin-left: 0.3em;">code: lat promoted to code: la </span> meaning "salted".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>When meat is industrially processed in preparation of consumption, it may be enriched with <a href="/wiki/Food_additive" title="Food additive">additives</a> to protect or modify its flavor or color, to improve its tenderness, juiciness or cohesiveness, or to aid with its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meat_preservation" title="Meat preservation">preservation</a>. Meat additives include the following:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_Additives_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_Additives-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">Salt</a> is the most frequently used additive in meat processing. It imparts flavor but also inhibits microbial growth, extends the product's shelf life and helps <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emulsifier" title="Emulsifier">emulsifying</a> finely processed products, such as sausages. Ready-to-eat meat products normally contain about 1.5 to 2.5 percent salt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_Additives_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_Additives-76">[76]</a></sup> Salt water or similar substances may also be injected into <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a> meat to improve the taste and increase the weight, in a process called <a href="/wiki/Plumping" title="Plumping">plumping</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nitrite" title="Nitrite">Nitrite</a> is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curing_meat" title="Curing meat">curing meat</a> to stabilize the meat's color and flavor, and inhibits the growth of spore-forming microorganisms such as <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridium_botulinum" title="Clostridium botulinum">C. botulinum</a></i>. The use of nitrite's precursor <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a> is now limited to a few products such as dry sausage, <a href="/wiki/Prosciutto" title="Prosciutto">prosciutto</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parma_ham" title="Parma ham">parma ham</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_Additives_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_Additives-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">Phosphates</a> used in meat processing are normally alkaline <a href="/wiki/Polyphosphate" title="Polyphosphate">polyphosphates</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sodium_tripolyphosphate" title="Sodium tripolyphosphate">sodium tripolyphosphate</a>. They are used to increase the water-binding and emulsifying ability of meat proteins, but also limit lipid oxidation and flavor loss, and reduce microbial growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_Additives_76-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_Additives-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erythorbate" title="Erythorbate">Erythorbate</a> or its equivalent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ascorbic_acid" title="Ascorbic acid">ascorbic acid</a> (vitamin C) is used to stabilize the color of cured meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_Additives_76-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_Additives-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweeteners" title="Sweeteners">Sweeteners</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Sugar" title="Sugar">sugar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Corn_syrup" title="Corn syrup">corn syrup</a> impart a sweet flavor, bind water and assist surface browning during cooking in the <a href="/wiki/Maillard_reaction" title="Maillard reaction">Maillard reaction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_Additives_76-5"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_Additives-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Seasoning" title="Seasoning">Seasonings</a> impart or modify flavor. They include <a href="/wiki/Spice" title="Spice">spices</a> or <a href="/wiki/Oleoresin" title="Oleoresin">oleoresins</a> extracted from them, <a href="/wiki/Herb" title="Herb">herbs</a>, vegetables and <a href="/wiki/Essential_oil" title="Essential oil">essential oils</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_Additives_76-6"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_Additives-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Flavoring" title="Flavoring">Flavorings</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Monosodium_glutamate" title="Monosodium glutamate">monosodium glutamate</a> impart or strengthen a particular flavor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_Additives_76-7"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_Additives-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenderizing" title="Tenderizing">Tenderizers</a> break down <a href="/wiki/Collagen" title="Collagen">collagens</a> to make the meat more palatable for consumption. They include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proteolytic_enzyme" title="Proteolytic enzyme">proteolytic enzymes</a>, acids, salt and phosphate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_Additives_76-8"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_Additives-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li>Dedicated <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial" title="Antimicrobial">antimicrobials</a> include <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid" title="Citric acid">citric</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_diacetate" title="Sodium diacetate">sodium diacetate</a>, acidified <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> or <a href="/wiki/Calcium_sulfate" title="Calcium sulfate">calcium sulfate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cetylpyridinium_chloride" title="Cetylpyridinium chloride">cetylpyridinium chloride</a>, activated <a href="/wiki/Lactoferrin" title="Lactoferrin">lactoferrin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodium_lactate" title="Sodium lactate">sodium</a> or <a href="/wiki/Potassium_lactate" title="Potassium lactate">potassium lactate</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Bacteriocin" title="Bacteriocin">bacteriocins</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Nisin" title="Nisin">nisin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_Additives_76-9"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_Additives-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">Antioxidants</a> include a wide range of chemicals that limit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lipid_oxidation" title="Lipid oxidation">lipid oxidation</a>, which creates an undesirable "off flavor", in precooked meat products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_Additives_76-10"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_Additives-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Acidifier" title="Acidifier">Acidifiers</a>, most often lactic or citric acid, can impart a tangy or tart flavor note, extend shelf-life, tenderize fresh meat or help with protein <a href="/wiki/Denaturation_(biochemistry)" title="Denaturation (biochemistry)">denaturation</a> and moisture release in dried meat. They substitute for the process of natural fermentation that acidifies some meat products such as hard <a href="/wiki/Salami" title="Salami">salami</a> or prosciutto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills,_Additives_76-11"><a href="#cite_note-Mills,_Additives-76">[76]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Misidentification">Misidentification</span></h3>
<p>With the rise of complex <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supply_chains" title="Supply chains">supply chains</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Cold_chain" title="Cold chain">cold chains</a>, in developed economies, the distance between the farmer or fisherman and customer has grown, increasing the possibility for intentional and unintentional misidentification of meat at various points in the supply chain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPFish_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-WPFish-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2013, reports emerged across Europe that products labelled as containing beef <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2013_meat_adulteration_scandal" title="2013 meat adulteration scandal">actually contained horse meat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> In February 2013 a study was published showing that about one-third of raw fish are misidentified across the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WPFish_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-WPFish-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Imitation">Imitation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meat_analogue" title="Meat analogue">Meat analogue</a></div>
<p>Various forms of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imitation_meat" title="Imitation meat">imitation meat</a> have been created for people who wish not to eat meat but still want to taste its flavor and texture. Meat imitates are typically some form of processed <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">soybean</a> (<a href="/wiki/Tofu" title="Tofu">tofu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tempeh" title="Tempeh">tempeh</a>), but they can also be based on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wheat_gluten_(food)" title="Wheat gluten (food)">wheat gluten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pea_protein" title="Pea protein">pea protein isolate</a>, or even fungi (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quorn_(food_product)" title="Quorn (food product)">quorn</a>).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_meat_production" title="Environmental impact of meat production">Environmental impact of meat production</a></div>
<p>Various environmental effects are associated with meat production. Among these are greenhouse gas emissions, fossil energy use, water use, water quality changes, and effects on grazed ecosystems.
</p><p>The livestock sector may be the largest source of <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a> (due to animal wastes, fertilizers, pesticides), and it contributes to emergence of antibiotic resistance. It accounts for over 8% of global human water use. It is a significant driver of <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">biodiversity loss</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, as it causes <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> and requires large amounts of land for pasture and feed crops, ocean <a href="/wiki/Dead_zone_(ecology)" title="Dead zone (ecology)">dead zones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">land degradation</a>, pollution, <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Machovina_2015_419–431_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Machovina_2015_419–431-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1038/s43016-021-00358-x_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1038/s43016-021-00358-x-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The occurrence, nature and significance of environmental effects varies among livestock production systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Grazing of livestock can be beneficial for some wildlife species, but not for others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Targeted grazing of livestock is used as a food-producing alternative to herbicide use in some vegetation management.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_use">Land use</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cereals_allocated_to_food,_animal_feed_and_fuel,_World.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3400" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Cereals_allocated_to_food%2C_animal_feed_and_fuel%2C_World.png/220px-Cereals_allocated_to_food%2C_animal_feed_and_fuel%2C_World.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Cereals_allocated_to_food%2C_animal_feed_and_fuel%2C_World.png/330px-Cereals_allocated_to_food%2C_animal_feed_and_fuel%2C_World.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Cereals_allocated_to_food%2C_animal_feed_and_fuel%2C_World.png/440px-Cereals_allocated_to_food%2C_animal_feed_and_fuel%2C_World.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cereals_allocated_to_food,_animal_feed_and_fuel,_World.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cereal-use statistic showing an estimated large fraction of crops used as fodder</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opera%C3%A7%C3%A3o_Hymenaea,_Julho-2016_(29399454651).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Opera%C3%A7%C3%A3o_Hymenaea%2C_Julho-2016_%2829399454651%29.jpg/220px-Opera%C3%A7%C3%A3o_Hymenaea%2C_Julho-2016_%2829399454651%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Opera%C3%A7%C3%A3o_Hymenaea%2C_Julho-2016_%2829399454651%29.jpg/330px-Opera%C3%A7%C3%A3o_Hymenaea%2C_Julho-2016_%2829399454651%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Opera%C3%A7%C3%A3o_Hymenaea%2C_Julho-2016_%2829399454651%29.jpg/440px-Opera%C3%A7%C3%A3o_Hymenaea%2C_Julho-2016_%2829399454651%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Opera%C3%A7%C3%A3o_Hymenaea,_Julho-2016_(29399454651).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Meat production is a main driver of tropical deforestation, in the Amazon largely <a href="/wiki/Beef#Environmental_impact" title="Beef">due to beef</a> production/<a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">exports</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Meat production is by far the biggest cause of land use, as it accounts for nearly 40% of the global land surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Just in the contiguous United States, 34% of its land area (265 million hectares or 654 million acres) are used as pasture and rangeland, mostly feeding livestock, not counting 158 million hectares (391 million acres) of cropland (20%), some of which is used for producing feed for livestock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Roughly 75% of <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforested</a> land around the globe is used for <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a> <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pasture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Deforestation from practices like <a href="/wiki/Slash-and-burn" title="Slash-and-burn">slash-and-burn</a> releases CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> and removes the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">carbon sink</a> of grown tropical forest ecosystems which substantially mitigate climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> The land use is a major pressure on pressure on <a href="/wiki/Soil_conservation" title="Soil conservation">fertile soils</a> which is important for global <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_change">Climate change</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Livestock%27s_Long_Shadow" title="Livestock's Long Shadow">Livestock's Long Shadow</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Environmental-impact-of-food-by-life-cycle-stage.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2635" data-file-width="2933" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Environmental-impact-of-food-by-life-cycle-stage.png/220px-Environmental-impact-of-food-by-life-cycle-stage.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Environmental-impact-of-food-by-life-cycle-stage.png/330px-Environmental-impact-of-food-by-life-cycle-stage.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Environmental-impact-of-food-by-life-cycle-stage.png/440px-Environmental-impact-of-food-by-life-cycle-stage.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Environmental-impact-of-food-by-life-cycle-stage.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Life-cycle_assessment" title="Life-cycle assessment">Life-cycle assessment</a> of GHG emissions for foods. Beef is the food with the largest carbon footprint, mainly due to <a href="/wiki/Methane_emissions" title="Methane emissions">methane production from cows</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The rising global consumption of <a href="/wiki/Emission_intensity" title="Emission intensity">carbon-intensive</a> meat products has "exploded the global carbon footprint of agriculture," according to some top scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Meat production is responsible for 14.5% and possibly up to 51% of the world's anthropogenic greenhouse gas emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="51% is a ridiculous number and there should be more recent numbers in concensus documents like ipcc (January 2021)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup> Some nations show very different impacts to counterparts within the same group, with Brazil and Australia having emissions over 200% higher than the average of their respective income groups and driven by meat consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Assessing_the_Environmental_Impacts_of_Consumption_and_Production" title="Assessing the Environmental Impacts of Consumption and Production">Assessing the Environmental Impacts of Consumption and Production</a></i> report produced by <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a>'s (UNEP) international panel for sustainable resource management, a worldwide transition in the direction of a meat and dairy free diet is indispensable if adverse global climate change were to be prevented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TG-20100602_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-TG-20100602-104">[104]</a></sup> A 2019 report in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lancet" title="The Lancet">The Lancet</a></i> recommended that global meat (and sugar) consumption be reduced by 50 percent to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">mitigate climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Meat consumption in Western societies needs to be reduced by up to 90% according to a 2018 study published in <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-107">[107]</a></sup> The 2019 special report by the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a> called for significantly reducing meat consumption, particularly in wealthy countries, in order to mitigate and adapt to climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1038/d41586-019-02409-7_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1038/d41586-019-02409-7-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity_loss">Biodiversity loss</span></h3>
<p>Meat consumption is considered one of the primary contributors of the <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">sixth mass extinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Machovina_2015_419–431_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Machovina_2015_419–431-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zalasiewicz2015_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zalasiewicz2015-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> A 2017 study by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" title="World Wildlife Fund">World Wildlife Fund</a> found that 60% of global <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">biodiversity loss</a> is attributable to meat-based diets, in particular from the vast scale of feed crop cultivation needed to rear tens of billions of farm animals for human consumption puts an enormous strain on natural resources resulting in a wide-scale loss of lands and species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Currently, livestock make up 60% of the <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">biomass</a> of all mammals on earth, followed by humans (36%) and wild mammals (4%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> In November 2017, 15,364 world scientists signed a <a href="/wiki/World_Scientists%27_Warning_to_Humanity" title="World Scientists' Warning to Humanity">Warning to Humanity</a> calling for, among other things, drastically diminishing our per capita consumption of meat and "dietary shifts towards mostly plant-based foods".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The 2019 <i><a href="/wiki/Global_Assessment_Report_on_Biodiversity_and_Ecosystem_Services" title="Global Assessment Report on Biodiversity and Ecosystem Services">Global Assessment Report on Biodiversity and Ecosystem Services</a></i>, released by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IPBES" title="IPBES">IPBES</a>, also recommended reductions in meat consumption in order to mitigate biodiversity loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> A 2021 <a href="/wiki/Chatham_House" title="Chatham House">Chatham House</a> report asserted that a significant shift towards plant-based diets would free up the land to allow for the restoration of ecosystems and thriving biodiversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carrington_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carrington-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>A July 2018 study in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> says that meat consumption is set to rise as the human population increases along with affluence, which will increase greenhouse gas emissions and further reduce biodiversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reducing_environmental_impact">Reducing environmental impact</span></h3>
<p>The environmental impact of meat production can be reduced by conversion of human-inedible residues of food crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Manure from meat-producing livestock is used as fertilizer; it may be composted before application to food crops. Substitution of animal manures for synthetic fertilizers in crop production can be environmentally significant, as between 43 and 88 MJ of fossil fuel energy are used per kg of nitrogen in manufacture of synthetic nitrogenous fertilizers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reducing_meat_consumption">Reducing meat consumption</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_consumption#Sustainable_food_consumption" title="Sustainable consumption">Sustainable consumption § Sustainable food consumption</a></div>
<p>The IPCC and many others, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_review" title="Scientific review">scientific reviews</a> of the literature and data on the topic, have concluded that meat production has to be reduced substantially for any sufficient <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">mitigation of</a> climate change and, at least initially, largely through <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_consumption#Shifting_consumption" title="Sustainable consumption">shifts</a> towards <a href="/wiki/Plant-based_diet" title="Plant-based diet">plant-based diets</a> in cases (e.g. countries) where meat consumption is high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1038/d41586-019-02409-7_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1038/d41586-019-02409-7-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-snmeat_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-snmeat-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-107">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1038/s43016-021-00431-5_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1038/s43016-021-00431-5-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.foodres.2020.109341_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.foodres.2020.109341-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1146/annurev-resource-111820-032340_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1146/annurev-resource-111820-032340-37">[37]</a></sup> A review names <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_meat_production#Meat_reduction_strategies" title="Environmental impact of meat production">broad potential measures</a> such as "restrictions or fiscal mechanisms".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1146/annurev-resource-111820-032340_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-10.1146/annurev-resource-111820-032340-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Personal_carbon_trading" title="Personal carbon trading">Personal Carbon Allowances</a> that <a href="/wiki/Rationing" title="Rationing">allow a certain amount</a> of free meat consumption per person would be a form of restriction, <a href="/wiki/Meat_tax" title="Meat tax">meat taxes</a> would be a type of fiscal mechanism. Meat can be replaced by, for example, high-protein iron-rich low-emission <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legumes</a> and common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, but there are also <a href="/wiki/Dietary_supplement" title="Dietary supplement">dietary supplements</a> (e.g. of vitamin B<sub>12</sub> and zinc) and/or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fortified_food" title="Fortified food">fortified foods</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cultured_meat" title="Cultured meat">cultured meat</a> (still under development), <a href="/wiki/Microbial_food_cultures" title="Microbial food cultures">microbial foods</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mycoprotein" title="Mycoprotein">mycoprotein</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/List_of_meat_substitutes" title="List of meat substitutes">meat substitutes</a>, and other alternatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.appet.2020.105058_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.appet.2020.105058-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Farms can be transitioned to meet new demands, workers can enter relevant job retraining programs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs additional references to reliable sources. (May 2022)">additional citation(s) needed</span></a></i>]</sup> and land previously used for meat production can be <a href="/wiki/Restoration_ecology" title="Restoration ecology">rewilded</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1038/s43016-021-00431-5_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1038/s43016-021-00431-5-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1038/s43016-021-00358-x_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1038/s43016-021-00358-x-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The biologists <a href="/wiki/Rodolfo_Dirzo" title="Rodolfo Dirzo">Rodolfo Dirzo</a>, Gerardo Ceballos, and <a href="/wiki/Paul_R._Ehrlich" title="Paul R. Ehrlich">Paul R. Ehrlich</a> emphasize that it is the "massive planetary monopoly of industrial meat production that needs to be curbed" while respecting the cultural traditions of indigenous peoples, for whom meat is an important source of protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spoilage_and_preservation">Spoilage and preservation</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Meat_spoilage" title="Meat spoilage">Meat spoilage</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Meat_preservation" title="Meat preservation">Meat preservation</a></div>
<p>The spoilage of meat occurs, if untreated, in a matter of hours or days and results in the meat becoming unappetizing, poisonous or infectious. Spoilage is caused by the practically unavoidable infection and subsequent <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decomposition</a> of meat by <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>, which are borne by the animal itself, by the people handling the meat, and by their implements. Meat can be kept edible for a much longer time – though not indefinitely – if proper <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a> is observed during production and processing, and if appropriate <a href="/wiki/Food_safety" title="Food safety">food safety</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_preservation" title="Food preservation">food preservation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Food_storage" title="Food storage">food storage</a> procedures are applied. Without the application of <a href="/wiki/Preservative" title="Preservative">preservatives</a> and stabilizers, the fats in meat may also begin to rapidly decompose after cooking or processing, leading to an objectionable taste known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warmed_over_flavor" title="Warmed over flavor">warmed over flavor</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods_of_preparation">Methods of preparation<span class="anchor" id="Processed_meat"></span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG/220px-Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG/330px-Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG/440px-Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hanging_Meat_at_a_Street_Fair_2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A spit <a href="/wiki/Barbecue" title="Barbecue">barbecue</a> at a street fair in New York City's <a href="/wiki/East_Village,_Manhattan" title="East Village, Manhattan">East Village</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Porksmoke1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Porksmoke1.jpg/220px-Porksmoke1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Porksmoke1.jpg/330px-Porksmoke1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Porksmoke1.jpg/440px-Porksmoke1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Porksmoke1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pork ribs being smoked</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Poronk%C3%A4ristys.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Poronk%C3%A4ristys.jpg/220px-Poronk%C3%A4ristys.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Poronk%C3%A4ristys.jpg/330px-Poronk%C3%A4ristys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Poronk%C3%A4ristys.jpg/440px-Poronk%C3%A4ristys.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Poronk%C3%A4ristys.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Saut%C3%A9ed_reindeer" title="Sautéed reindeer">Sautéed reindeer</a> with <a href="/wiki/Mashed_potato" title="Mashed potato">mashed potatoes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lingonberry" title="Lingonberry">lingonberry</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gherkin" title="Gherkin">gherkin</a></div></div></div>
<p>Fresh meat can be cooked for immediate consumption, or be processed, that is, treated for longer-term <a href="/wiki/Food_preservation" title="Food preservation">preservation</a> and later consumption, possibly after further preparation. Fresh meat cuts or processed cuts may produce iridescence, commonly thought to be due to spoilage but actually caused by structural coloration and <a href="/wiki/Diffraction" title="Diffraction">diffraction</a> of the light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-foods_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-foods-142">[142]</a></sup> A common additive to processed meats for both preservation and the prevention of discoloration is <a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrite" title="Sodium nitrite">sodium nitrite</a>. This substance is a source of health concerns because it may form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carcinogenic" title="Carcinogenic">carcinogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Nitrosamine" title="Nitrosamine">nitrosamines</a> when heated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meat is prepared in many ways, as <a href="/wiki/Steak" title="Steak">steaks</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Stew" title="Stew">stews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fondue" title="Fondue">fondue</a>, or as <a href="/wiki/Dried_meat" title="Dried meat">dried meat</a> like beef jerky. It may be ground then formed into patties (as <a href="/wiki/Hamburger" title="Hamburger">hamburgers</a> or croquettes), loaves, or <a href="/wiki/Sausage" title="Sausage">sausages</a>, or used in loose form (as in "sloppy joe" or <a href="/wiki/Bolognese_sauce" title="Bolognese sauce">Bolognese sauce</a>).
</p><p>Some meat is cured by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smoking_(food)" title="Smoking (food)">smoking</a>, which is the process of <a href="/wiki/Seasoning" title="Seasoning">flavoring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cooking" title="Cooking">cooking</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Food_preservation" title="Food preservation">preserving</a> <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a> by exposing it to the <a href="/wiki/Smoke" title="Smoke">smoke</a> from burning or smoldering plant materials, most often <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alder" title="Alder">alder</a> is the traditional smoking wood, but <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a> is more often used now, and <a href="/wiki/Beech" title="Beech">beech</a> to a lesser extent. In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hickory" title="Hickory">hickory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesquite" title="Mesquite">mesquite</a>, oak, <a href="/wiki/Pecan" title="Pecan">pecan</a>, alder, <a href="/wiki/Maple" title="Maple">maple</a>, and fruit-tree woods are commonly used for smoking. Meat can also be cured by <a href="/wiki/Pickling" title="Pickling">pickling</a>, preserving in <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a> or <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salted_meat" title="Salted meat">salted meat</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Curing_(food_preservation)" title="Curing (food preservation)">curing</a> methods). Other kinds of meat are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marinade" title="Marinade">marinated</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barbecue" title="Barbecue">barbecued</a>, or simply boiled, <a href="/wiki/Roasting" title="Roasting">roasted</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Frying" title="Frying">fried</a>.
</p><p>Meat is generally eaten cooked, but many recipes call for raw beef, veal or fish (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tartare" title="Tartare">tartare</a>). <a href="/wiki/Steak_tartare" title="Steak tartare">Steak tartare</a> is a meat dish made from finely chopped or minced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raw_food" title="Raw food">raw</a> <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a> or <a href="/wiki/Horse_meat" title="Horse meat">horse meat</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Meat is often spiced or seasoned, particularly with meat products such as sausages. Meat dishes are usually described by their source (animal and part of body) and method of preparation (e.g., a beef rib).
</p><p>Meat is a typical base for making <a href="/wiki/Sandwich" title="Sandwich">sandwiches</a>. Popular varieties of sandwich meat include <a href="/wiki/Ham" title="Ham">ham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salami" title="Salami">salami</a> and other sausages, and <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Steak" title="Steak">steak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roast_beef" title="Roast beef">roast beef</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corned_beef" title="Corned beef">corned beef</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pepperoni" title="Pepperoni">pepperoni</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pastrami" title="Pastrami">pastrami</a>. Meat can also be molded or pressed (common for products that include offal, such as <a href="/wiki/Haggis" title="Haggis">haggis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scrapple" title="Scrapple">scrapple</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Canning" title="Canning">canned</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Red_meat#Health_effects" title="Red meat">Red meat § Health effects</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_meat_production#Meat_reduction_and_health" title="Environmental impact of meat production">Environmental impact of meat production § Meat reduction and health</a></div>
<p>There is concern and debate regarding the potential association of meat, in particular red and processed meat, with a variety of health risks. A study of 400,000 subjects conducted by the <a href="/wiki/European_Prospective_Investigation_into_Cancer_and_Nutrition" title="European Prospective Investigation into Cancer and Nutrition">European Prospective Investigation into Cancer and Nutrition</a> and published in 2013 showed "a moderate positive association between processed meat consumption and mortality, in particular due to cardiovascular diseases, but also to cancer."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMC03713_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMC03713-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, the <a href="/wiki/International_Agency_for_Research_on_Cancer" title="International Agency for Research on Cancer">International Agency for Research on Cancer</a> of the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) classified processed meat as <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogenic</a> to humans (Group 1), based on "sufficient evidence in humans that the consumption of processed meat causes <a href="/wiki/Colorectal_cancer" title="Colorectal cancer">colorectal cancer</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-who-147">[147]</a></sup> In the same year, the Agency classified red meat as <i>probably</i> (Group 2A) carcinogenic to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IARC2015PR_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-IARC2015PR-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>2015–2020 <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Guidelines_for_Americans" title="Dietary Guidelines for Americans">Dietary Guidelines for Americans</a></i> asked men and teenage boys to increase their consumption of vegetables or other underconsumed foods (fruits, whole grains, and dairy) while reducing intake of protein foods (meats, poultry, and eggs) that they currently overconsume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Risk_of_25_common_conditions_per_70_grams_per_day_higher_daily_intake_of_unprocessed_red_and_processed_meat.webp"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1518" data-file-width="1418" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Risk_of_25_common_conditions_per_70_grams_per_day_higher_daily_intake_of_unprocessed_red_and_processed_meat.webp/220px-Risk_of_25_common_conditions_per_70_grams_per_day_higher_daily_intake_of_unprocessed_red_and_processed_meat.webp.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Risk_of_25_common_conditions_per_70_grams_per_day_higher_daily_intake_of_unprocessed_red_and_processed_meat.webp/330px-Risk_of_25_common_conditions_per_70_grams_per_day_higher_daily_intake_of_unprocessed_red_and_processed_meat.webp.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Risk_of_25_common_conditions_per_70_grams_per_day_higher_daily_intake_of_unprocessed_red_and_processed_meat.webp/440px-Risk_of_25_common_conditions_per_70_grams_per_day_higher_daily_intake_of_unprocessed_red_and_processed_meat.webp.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Risk_of_25_common_conditions_per_70_grams_per_day_higher_daily_intake_of_unprocessed_red_and_processed_meat.webp" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Risk of 25 common conditions per &gt;70 g/day daily intake of unprocessed red and processed meat according to a study using <a href="/wiki/UK_Biobank" title="UK Biobank">UK Biobank</a> data<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1186/s12916-021-01922-9_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1186/s12916-021-01922-9-150">[150]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Health authorities around the world recommend limiting consumption of unprocessed red meat (such as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beef_steak" title="Beef steak">beef steak</a>) and also discourage consumption of <a href="/wiki/Processed_meat" title="Processed meat">processed meat</a> (such as bacon).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John2019_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-John2019-151">[151]</a></sup> In 2021, a study of data on half a million U.K. citizens shows associations between high levels of meat intake with risks of some of 25 common conditions including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ischaemic_heart_disease" title="Ischaemic heart disease">ischaemic heart disease</a> and diabetes as well as a lower risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_deficiency_anaemia" title="Iron deficiency anaemia">iron deficiency anaemia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1186/s12916-021-01922-9_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1186/s12916-021-01922-9-150">[150]</a></sup> A cohort study with over 130,000 participants published a few days later, also found that a higher intake of processed meat was associated with "a higher risk of mortality and major CVD".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> However, while some of the results did control for <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a> various other factors that were not controlled for may confound the associations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1186/s12916-021-01922-9_150-2"><a href="#cite_note-10.1186/s12916-021-01922-9-150">[150]</a></sup> and research of underlying mechanisms may be required for fully robust conclusions. Studies have however concluded that <a href="/wiki/Plant-based_diet" title="Plant-based diet">plant-based diets</a> "rich in legumes, whole grains, and nuts with reduced red and processed meats" and low in overall meat consumption (except for fish) <a href="/wiki/Healthy_diet#Optimal_diet" title="Healthy diet">are associated with longer life expectancy</a>, whereby a switch from a "typical Western diet" in adults can <a href="/wiki/Life_extension" title="Life extension">increase life expectancy</a> by a decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contamination">Contamination</span></h3>
<p>Various toxic compounds can contaminate meat, including <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mycotoxin" title="Mycotoxin">mycotoxins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticide</a> residues, <a href="/wiki/Dioxin" title="Dioxin">dioxins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyl" title="Polychlorinated biphenyl">polychlorinated biphenyl</a> (PCBs). Processed, smoked and cooked meat may contain <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogens</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbons" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAHs_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-PAHs-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>Toxins may be introduced to meat as part of animal feed, as veterinary drug residues, or during processing and cooking. Often, these compounds can be metabolized in the body to form harmful by-products. Negative effects depend on the individual genome, diet, and history of the consumer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Püssa_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-Püssa-157">[157]</a></sup> Any chemical's toxicity is also dependent on the dose and timing of exposure.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer">Cancer</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Red_meat#Cancer" title="Red meat">Red meat § Cancer</a></div>
<p>There are concerns about a relationship between the consumption of meat, in particular processed and red meat, and increased <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> risk. The <a href="/wiki/International_Agency_for_Research_on_Cancer" title="International Agency for Research on Cancer">International Agency for Research on Cancer</a> (IARC), a specialized agency of the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO), classified processed meat (e.g., bacon, ham, hot dogs, sausages) as, "<i>carcinogenic to humans</i> (Group 1), based on <i>sufficient evidence</i> in humans that the consumption of processed meat causes colorectal cancer." IARC also classified red meat as "<i>probably carcinogenic to humans</i> (Group 2A), based on <i>limited evidence</i> that the consumption of red meat causes cancer in humans and <i>strong</i> mechanistic evidence supporting a carcinogenic effect."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-20151026_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-20151026-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20151026_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151026-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC-20151026_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20151026-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heart_disease">Heart disease</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Relative_risk_and_95%25_confidence_intervals_of_ischemic_heart_disease_for_each_50_g_per_day_increase_in_processed_meat_consumption.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1201" data-file-width="1867" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Relative_risk_and_95%25_confidence_intervals_of_ischemic_heart_disease_for_each_50_g_per_day_increase_in_processed_meat_consumption.jpg/220px-Relative_risk_and_95%25_confidence_intervals_of_ischemic_heart_disease_for_each_50_g_per_day_increase_in_processed_meat_consumption.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Relative_risk_and_95%25_confidence_intervals_of_ischemic_heart_disease_for_each_50_g_per_day_increase_in_processed_meat_consumption.jpg/330px-Relative_risk_and_95%25_confidence_intervals_of_ischemic_heart_disease_for_each_50_g_per_day_increase_in_processed_meat_consumption.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Relative_risk_and_95%25_confidence_intervals_of_ischemic_heart_disease_for_each_50_g_per_day_increase_in_processed_meat_consumption.jpg/440px-Relative_risk_and_95%25_confidence_intervals_of_ischemic_heart_disease_for_each_50_g_per_day_increase_in_processed_meat_consumption.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Relative_risk_and_95%25_confidence_intervals_of_ischemic_heart_disease_for_each_50_g_per_day_increase_in_processed_meat_consumption.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Risk of ischemic heart disease for each 50 g per day increase in processed meat consumption<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1080/10408398.2021.1949575_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1080/10408398.2021.1949575-161">[161]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The correlation of consumption to increased risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heart_disease" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a> is controversial. Some studies fail to find a link between red meat consumption and heart disease<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> (although the same study found statistically significant correlation between the consumption of processed meat and coronary heart disease). A large cohort study of Seventh-Day Adventists in <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> found that the risk of heart disease is three times greater for 45-64-year-old men who eat meat daily, versus those who did not eat meat. This study compared adventists to the general population and not other Seventh Day Adventists who ate meat and did not specifically distinguish red and processed meat in its assessment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>A Harvard University study<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> in 2010 involving over one million people who ate meat found that only processed meat had an adverse risk in relation to coronary heart disease. The study suggests that eating 50 g (less than 2 ounces) of processed meat per day increases risk of coronary heart disease by 42%, and diabetes by 19%. Equivalent levels of fat, including saturated fats, in unprocessed meat (even when eating twice as much per day) did not show any deleterious effects, leading the researchers to suggest that "differences in salt and preservatives, rather than fats, might explain the higher risk of heart disease and diabetes seen with processed meats, but not with unprocessed red meats."
</p><p>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_review" title="Scientific review">scientific review</a> concluded that, except for poultry, at 50 g/day unprocessed red and processed meat appear to be risk factors for ischemic heart disease, increasing the risk by about 9 and 18% respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1080/10408398.2021.1949575_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1080/10408398.2021.1949575-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Obesity">Obesity</span></h3>
<p>Prospective analysis suggests that meat consumption is positively associated with weight gain in men and women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Cattlemen%27s_Beef_Association" title="National Cattlemen's Beef Association">National Cattlemen's Beef Association</a> countered by stating that meat consumption may not be associated with fat gain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> In response, the authors of the original study controlled for just abdominal fat across a sample of 91,214 people and found that even when controlling for calories and lifestyle factors, meat consumption is linked with obesity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> Additional studies and reviews have confirmed the finding that greater meat consumption is positively linked with greater weight gain even when controlling for calories, and lifestyle factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bacterial_contamination">Bacterial contamination</span></h3>
<p>Bacterial contamination has been seen with meat products. A 2011 study by the <a href="/wiki/Translational_Genomics_Research_Institute" title="Translational Genomics Research Institute">Translational Genomics Research Institute</a> showed that nearly half (47%) of the meat and <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a> in U.S. grocery stores were contaminated with <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Staphylococcus aureus">S. aureus</a></i>, with more than half (52%) of those bacteria resistant to antibiotics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> A 2018 investigation by the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Investigative_Journalism" title="Bureau of Investigative Journalism">Bureau of Investigative Journalism</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i> found that around 15 percent of the US population suffers from foodborne illnesses every year. The investigation also highlighted unsanitary conditions in US-based meat plants, which included meat products covered in excrement and abscesses "filled with pus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infectious_diseases">Infectious diseases</span></h3>
<p>Meat production and trade substantially increases risks for infectious diseases, <a href="/wiki/Pandemic_prevention" title="Pandemic prevention">including of pandemics</a> – "directly through increased contact with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wild_meat" title="Wild meat">wild</a> and farmed animals [(<a href="/wiki/Zoonosis" title="Zoonosis">zoonosis</a>)] or indirectly through <a href="#Environmental_impact">its impact on the environment</a> (e.g., biodiversity loss, water use, climate change)".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1016/j.foodres.2020.109341_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1016/j.foodres.2020.109341-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Avian_influenza" title="Avian influenza">avian influenza</a> from poultry meat production can be a threat to human health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Furthermore, the use of antibiotics in meat production contributes to <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_resistance" title="Antimicrobial resistance">antimicrobial resistance</a> – which contributes to millions of deaths<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:8_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-:8-181">[181]</a></sup> – and makes it harder to control infectious diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_in_consumer_behavior">Changes in consumer behavior</span></h3>
<p>In response to changing prices as well as health concerns about saturated fat and cholesterol (see <a href="/wiki/Lipid_hypothesis" title="Lipid hypothesis">lipid hypothesis</a>), consumers have altered their consumption of various meats. A <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">USDA</a> report points out that consumption of beef in the United States between 1970–1974 and 1990–1994 dropped by 21%, while consumption of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicken_(food)" title="Chicken (food)">chicken</a> increased by 90%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> During the same period of time, the price of chicken dropped by 14% relative to the price of beef. From 1995 to 1996, beef consumption increased due to <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">higher supplies and lower prices</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cooking">Cooking</span></h3>
<p>Meat can transmit certain <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">diseases</a>, but complete cooking and avoiding recontamination reduces this possibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several studies published since 1990 indicate that cooking muscle meat creates <a href="/wiki/Heterocyclic_amine" title="Heterocyclic amine">heterocyclic amines</a> (HCAs), which are thought to increase cancer risk in humans. Researchers at the National Cancer Institute published results of a study which found that human subjects who ate beef rare or medium-rare had less than one third the risk of stomach cancer than those who ate beef medium-well or well-done.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> While eating muscle meat raw may be the only way to avoid HCAs fully, the <a href="/wiki/National_Cancer_Institute" title="National Cancer Institute">National Cancer Institute</a> states that cooking meat below 100 °C (212 °F) creates "negligible amounts" of HCAs. Also, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microwaving" title="Microwaving">microwaving</a> meat before cooking may reduce HCAs by 90%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nitrosamine" title="Nitrosamine">Nitrosamines</a>, present in processed and cooked foods, have been noted as being carcinogenic, being linked to colon cancer. Also, toxic compounds called PAHs, or <a href="/wiki/Polycyclic_aromatic_hydrocarbon" title="Polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbon">polycyclic aromatic hydrocarbons</a>, present in processed, smoked and cooked foods, are known to be carcinogenic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PAHs_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-PAHs-156">[156]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sociology">Sociology</span></h2>
<p>Meat is part of the human diet in most cultures, where it often has symbolic meaning and important social functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> Some people choose not to eat meat (<a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism" title="Vegetarianism">vegetarianism</a>) or any food made from animals (<a href="/wiki/Veganism" title="Veganism">veganism</a>). The reasons for not eating all or some meat may include ethical objections to killing animals for food, health concerns, environmental concerns or religious <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_laws" title="Dietary laws">dietary laws</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics">Ethics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_eating_meat" title="Ethics of eating meat">Ethics of eating meat</a></div>
<p>Ethical issues regarding the consumption of meat include objecting to the act of killing animals or to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intensive_agriculture" title="Intensive agriculture">agricultural practices</a> used in meat production. Reasons for objecting to killing animals for consumption may include <a href="/wiki/Animal_rights" title="Animal rights">animal rights</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_ethics" title="Environmental ethics">environmental ethics</a>, or an aversion to inflicting <a href="/wiki/Pain" title="Pain">pain</a> or harm on other <a href="/wiki/Sentience" title="Sentience">sentient creatures</a>. Some people, while not vegetarians, refuse to eat the flesh of certain animals (such as cows, pigs, cats, dogs, horses, or rabbits) due to cultural or religious traditions.
</p><p>Some people eat only the flesh of animals that they believe have not been mistreated, and abstain from the flesh of animals raised in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Factory_farming" title="Factory farming">factory farms</a> or else abstain from particular products, such as <a href="/wiki/Foie_gras" title="Foie gras">foie gras</a> and <a href="/wiki/Veal" title="Veal">veal</a>.
</p><p>Some techniques of intensive agriculture may be cruel to animals: foie gras is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_product" title="Food product">food product</a> made from the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> of <a href="/wiki/Domestic_duck" title="Domestic duck">ducks</a> or <a href="/wiki/Domestic_goose" title="Domestic goose">geese</a> that have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Force_feeding" title="Force feeding">force fed</a> corn to fatten the organ; veal is criticised because the veal calves may be highly restricted in movement, have unsuitable flooring, spend their entire lives indoors, experience prolonged deprivation (sensory, social, and exploratory), and be more susceptible to high amounts of stress and disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_traditions">Religious traditions</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism_and_religion" title="Vegetarianism and religion">Vegetarianism and religion</a></div>
<p>The religion of <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> has always opposed eating meat, and there are also <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_vegetarianism" title="Buddhist vegetarianism">schools of Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism#Vegetarian_Hindus" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> that condemn the eating of meat.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> dietary rules (<i><a href="/wiki/Kashrut" title="Kashrut">Kashrut</a></i>) allow certain (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kosher" title="Kosher">kosher</a></i>) meat and forbid other (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treif" title="Treif">treif</a></i>). The rules include prohibitions on the consumption of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unclean_animals" title="Unclean animals">unclean animals</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a> including <a href="/wiki/Mollusca" title="Mollusca">mollusca</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustacea" title="Crustacea">crustacea</a>, and most <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>), and mixtures of meat and milk.
</p><p>Similar rules apply in <a href="/wiki/Islamic_dietary_laws" title="Islamic dietary laws">Islamic dietary laws</a>: The <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> explicitly forbids meat from animals that die naturally, blood, the meat of swine (porcine animals, pigs), and animals dedicated to other than Allah (either undedicated or dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idols</a>) which are <a href="/wiki/Haram" title="Haram">haram</a> as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Halal" title="Halal">halal</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a> forbids meat of slowly slaughtered animals ("<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kutha_meat" title="Kutha meat">kutha</a>") and prescribes killing animals with a single strike ("<a href="/wiki/Jhatka" title="Jhatka">jhatka</a>"), but some Sikh groups oppose eating any meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Takhar2005_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Takhar2005-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychology">Psychology</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Psychology_of_eating_meat" title="Psychology of eating meat">Psychology of eating meat</a></div>
<p>Research in <a href="/wiki/Applied_psychology" title="Applied psychology">applied psychology</a> has investigated practices of meat eating in relation to <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emotion" title="Emotion">emotions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Personality" title="Personality">personality</a> characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loughnan2014_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loughnan2014-193">[193]</a></sup> Psychological research suggests meat eating is correlated with <a href="/wiki/Masculinity" title="Masculinity">masculinity</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rozin2012_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rozin2012-194">[194]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Social_dominance_orientation" title="Social dominance orientation">support for social hierarchy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> and reduced <a href="/wiki/Big_Five_personality_traits#Openness_to_experience" title="Big Five personality traits">openness to experience</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keller2015_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keller2015-196">[196]</a></sup> Research into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Consumer_psychology" title="Consumer psychology">consumer psychology</a> of meat is relevant both to <a href="/wiki/Meat_industry" title="Meat industry">meat industry</a> <a href="/wiki/Marketing" title="Marketing">marketing</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richardson1994_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richardson1994-197">[197]</a></sup> and to advocates of reduced meat consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klöckner_2014_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-Klöckner_2014-198">[198]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schösler_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schösler-199">[199]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender">Gender</span></h3>
<p>Unlike most other food, meat is not perceived as <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a>-neutral, and is particularly associated with men and <a href="/wiki/Masculinity" title="Masculinity">masculinity</a>. Sociological research, ranging from African tribal societies to contemporary <a href="/wiki/Barbecue" title="Barbecue">barbecues</a>, indicates that men are much more likely to participate in preparing meat than other food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 15">: 15 </span></sup> This has been attributed to the influence of traditional male <a href="/wiki/Gender_role" title="Gender role">gender roles</a>, in view of a "male familiarity with killing" (<a href="/wiki/Jack_Goody" title="Jack Goody">Goody</a>) or <a href="/wiki/Roasting" title="Roasting">roasting</a> being more violent as opposed to <a href="/wiki/Boiling" title="Boiling">boiling</a> (<a href="/wiki/Claude_L%C3%A9vi-Strauss" title="Claude Lévi-Strauss">Lévi-Strauss</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 15">: 15 </span></sup> By and large, at least in modern societies, men also tend to consume more meat than women, and men often prefer red meat whereas women tend to prefer chicken and fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 16">: 16 </span></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h4>
<p>The founders of <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western philosophy</a> disagreed about the <a href="/wiki/Ethics_of_eating_meat" title="Ethics of eating meat">ethics of eating meat</a>. <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Republic_(Plato)" title="Republic (Plato)">Republic</a></i> has <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> describe the ideal state as vegetarian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 10">: 10 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a> believed that humans and animals were equal and therefore disapproved of meat consumption, as did <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>, whereas <a href="/wiki/Zeno_of_Elea" title="Zeno of Elea">Zeno</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epicurus" title="Epicurus">Epicurus</a> were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetarian" title="Vegetarian">vegetarian</a> but allowed meat-eating in their philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-6"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 10">: 10 </span></sup> Conversely, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Politics_(Aristotle)" title="Politics (Aristotle)">Politics</a></i> assert that animals, as inferior beings,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> exist to serve humans, including as food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-7"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 10">: 10 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustine" title="Augustine">Augustine</a> drew on Aristotle to argue that the universe's natural hierarchy allows humans to eat animals, and animals to eat plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-8"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 10">: 10 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> philosophers were likewise divided. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes" title="Descartes">Descartes</a> wrote that animals are merely animated machines, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kant" title="Kant">Kant</a> considered them inferior beings for lack of discernment; means rather than ends.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-9"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 11">: 11 </span></sup> But <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> and <a class="mw-redirect"
