href="/wiki/Rousseau" title="Rousseau">Rousseau</a> disagreed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-10"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 11">: 11 </span></sup> The latter argued that meat-eating is a social rather than a natural act, because children are not interested in meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-11"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 11">: 11 </span></sup>
</p><p>Later philosophers examined the changing practices of eating meat in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_age" title="Modern age">modern age</a> as part of a process of detachment from animals as living beings. <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Elias" title="Norbert Elias">Norbert Elias</a>, for instance, noted that in medieval times cooked animals were brought to the table whole, but that since the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> only the edible parts are served, which are no longer recognizably part of an animal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-12"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 12">: 12 </span></sup> Modern eaters, according to <a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/No%C3%ABlie_Vialles" title="fr:Noëlie Vialles">Noëlie Vialles</a>, demand an "<a href="/wiki/Ellipsis_(linguistics)" title="Ellipsis (linguistics)">ellipsis</a>" between meat and dead animals; for instance, calves' eyes are no longer considered a delicacy as in the Middle Ages, but provoke disgust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-13"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 12">: 12 </span></sup> Even in the English language, distinctions emerged between animals and their meat, such as between <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pork" title="Pork">pork</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-14"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 12">: 12 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fernand_Braudel" title="Fernand Braudel">Fernand Braudel</a> wrote that since the European diet of the 15th and 16th century was particularly heavy in meat, European <a href="/wiki/Colonialism" title="Colonialism">colonialism</a> helped export meat-eating across the globe, as colonized peoples took up the culinary habits of their colonizers, which they associated with wealth and power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buscemi_33-15"><a href="#cite_note-Buscemi-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 15">: 15 </span></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-200">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source" id="CITEREFAristotleJowett">Aristotle; Jowett, B. <i>The Politics</i> (in Greek and English). Ancient Greece. p. I. 8. 1256b. <q>First then we may observe in living creatures both a <a href="/wiki/Despotism" title="Despotism">despotical</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Constitution" title="Constitution">constitutional</a> rule; for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mind-body_dualism" title="Mind-body dualism">soul</a> rules the body with a despotical rule, whereas the intellect rules the appetites with a constitutional and royal rule. And it is clear that the rule of the soul over the body, and of the mind and the rational element over the passionate is natural and expedient; whereas the equality of the two or the rule of the inferior is always hurtful. The same is good for animals as well as men; for tame animals have a better nature than wild, and all tame animals are better off when they are ruled by man; for then they are preserved.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Politics&amp;rft.place=Ancient+Greece&amp;rft.pages=I.+8.+1256b&amp;rft.au=Aristotle&amp;rft.au=Jowett%2C+B.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMeat"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-201"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-201">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-foreign-lang-source" id="CITEREFAristotleJowett">Aristotle; Jowett, B. <i>The Politics</i> (in Greek and English). Ancient Greece. p. I. 8. 1256b. <q><a href="/wiki/Viviparity" title="Viviparity">viviparous</a> animals have up to a certain time a supply of food for their young in themselves, which is called <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>. In like manner we may infer that, after the birth of animals, plants exist for their sake, and that the other animals exist for the sake of man, the tame for use and food, the wild, if not all, at least the greater part of them, for food, and for the provision of clothing and various instruments. Now if nature makes nothing incomplete, and nothing in vain, the inference must be that she has made all animals and plants for the sake of man</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Politics&amp;rft.place=Ancient+Greece&amp;rft.pages=I.+8.+1256b&amp;rft.au=Aristotle&amp;rft.au=Jowett%2C+B.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMeat"></span></span>
</li>
